            <div class="article fulltext-view " itemprop="articleBody"><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><h1 id="article-title-1" itemprop="headline">Weight Gain After Breast Cancer Diagnosis and All-Cause Mortality: Systematic Review and Meta-Analysis</h1>
               <div class="contributors">
                  <ol class="contributor-list" id="contrib-group-1">
                     <li class="contributor" id="contrib-1" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"
                         itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span class="name" itemprop="name"><a class="name-search"
                              href="/search?author1=Mary+C.+Playdon&amp;sortspec=date&amp;submit=Submit">Mary C. Playdon</a></span>, 
                     </li>
                     <li class="contributor" id="contrib-2" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"
                         itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span class="name" itemprop="name"><a class="name-search"
                              href="/search?author1=Michael+B.+Bracken&amp;sortspec=date&amp;submit=Submit">Michael B. Bracken</a></span>, 
                     </li>
                     <li class="contributor" id="contrib-3" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"
                         itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span class="name" itemprop="name"><a class="name-search"

                     </li>
                     <li class="contributor" id="contrib-4" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"
                         itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span class="name" itemprop="name"><a class="name-search"
                              href="/search?author1=Jennifer+A.+Ligibel&amp;sortspec=date&amp;submit=Submit">Jennifer A. Ligibel</a></span>, 
                     </li>
                     <li class="contributor" id="contrib-5" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope"
                         itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span class="name" itemprop="name"><a class="name-search"

                     </li>
                     <li class="last" id="contrib-6"><span class="name"><a class="name-search"
                              href="/search?author1=Melinda+L.+Irwin&amp;sortspec=date&amp;submit=Submit">Melinda L. Irwin</a></span></li>
                  </ol>
                  <ol class="affiliation-list">
                     <li class="aff"><a id="aff-1" name="aff-1"></a><address>

                     </li>

                     </li>

                  <ul class="history-list">
                     <li xmlns:hwp="http://schema.highwire.org/Journal" class="received"

               </div>
               <div class="section abstract" id="abstract-1" itemprop="description">
                  <div class="section-nav">
                     <div class="nav-placeholder"> </div><a href="#sec-5" title="Methods" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  <h2>Abstract</h2>
                  <div id="sec-1" class="subsection">
                     <p id="p-1"><strong>Background:</strong> Overweight and obesity are associated with breast cancer mortality. However, the relationship between postdiagnosis weight
                        gain and mortality is unclear. We conducted a systematic review and meta-analysis of weight gain after breast cancer diagnosis
                        and breast cancer–specific, all-cause mortality and recurrence outcomes.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-2" class="subsection">
                     <p id="p-2"><strong>Methods:</strong> Electronic databases identified articles up through December 2014, including: PubMed (1966-present), EMBASE (1974-present),
                        CINAHL (1982-present), and Web of Science. Language and publication status were unrestricted. Cohort studies and clinical
                        trials measuring weight change after diagnosis and all-cause/breast cancer–specific mortality or recurrence were considered.
                        Participants were women age 18 years or older with stage I-IIIC breast cancer. Fixed effects analysis summarized the association
                        between weight gain (≥5.0% body weight) and all-cause mortality; all tests were two-sided.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-3" class="subsection">
                     <p id="p-3"><strong>Results:</strong> Twelve studies (n = 23 832) were included. Weight gain (≥5.0%) compared with maintenance (&lt;±5.0%) was associated with increased
                        all-cause mortality (hazard ratio [HR] = 1.12, 95% confidence interval [CI] = 1.03 to 1.22, <em>P</em> = .01, I<sup>2</sup> = 55.0%). Higher risk of mortality was apparent for weight gain ≥10.0% (HR = 1.23, 95% CI = 1.09 to 1.39, <em>P</em> &lt; .001); 5% to 10.0% weight gain was not associated with all-cause mortality (<em>P</em> = .40). The association was not statistically significant for those with a prediagnosis body mass index (BMI) of less than
                        25kg/m<sup>2</sup> (HR = 1.14, 95% CI = 0.99 to 1.31, <em>P</em> = .07) or with a BMI of 25kg/m<sup>2</sup> or higher (HR = 1.00, 95% CI = 0.86 to 1.16, <em>P</em> = .19). Weight gain of 10.0% or more was not associated with hazard of breast cancer–specific mortality (HR = 1.17, 95% CI
                        = 1.00 to 1.38, <em>P</em> = .05).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-4" class="subsection">
                     <p id="p-4"><strong>Conclusions:</strong> Weight gain after diagnosis of breast cancer is associated with higher all-cause mortality rates compared with maintaining
                        body weight. Adverse effects are greater for weight gains of 10.0% or higher.
                     </p>
                  </div>
               </div>
               <p id="p-5">Breast cancer is the most common cancer in women in the United States besides nonmelanoma skin cancer and accounts for the
                  majority of cancer deaths after lung and bronchial cancer (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>,<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). With earlier detection, more targeted treatment, and an aging population, the number of women living with a diagnosis of
                  breast cancer continues to increase (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Being overweight or obese, characterized by having a body mass index (BMI) at or above 25kg/m<sup>2</sup> or a waist-hip ratio of 0.85cm or higher for women is associated with an increased risk for postmenopausal breast cancer
                  incidence and recurrence, breast cancer–specific and all-cause mortality in prospective, observational studies (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4–10</a>); the relationship with mortality differs by race/ethnicity (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>,<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). Weight gain throughout adult life is also associated with higher risk for developing breast cancer, particularly estrogen
                  and progesterone receptor (ER/PR)–positive cancer (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13–17</a>).
               </p>
               <p id="p-6" class="indent">Evidence including a systematic review and meta-analysis of 82 studies suggests that obesity is also associated with breast
                  cancer incidence and mortality in premenopausal women (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>,<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>), even after adjustment for methodological biases and stratification by histological subtype (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>,<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>,<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). In a recent analysis of data from 80 000 women with early-stage breast cancer participating in 70 trials with average follow-up
                  of eight years, obesity was strongly associated with breast cancer mortality, but only among premenopausal women with ER-positive
                  disease (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). Collectively, these findings suggest that breast cancer survivors of all ages who are obese might be at higher risk of
                  mortality, compared with women in the normal weight range, and highlights the importance of future randomized, controlled
                  investigations of the effects of weight loss intervention on survival outcomes in this population.
               </p>
               <p id="p-7" class="indent">During and after treatment for breast cancer the majority of women experience weight gain (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23–27</a>). However, the relationship between postdiagnosis weight gain and breast cancer mortality is unclear. Large observational
                  studies show conflicting findings as a result of methodological limitations and differences in timing of exposure and other
                  prognostic characteristics at baseline (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28–34</a>). Variation in magnitude of weight gain may be attributed to differences in treatment (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>), physical activity (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>), age, smoking status (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>), and length of follow-up, which reflects different points in the cancer trajectory. Generally, there is a pattern of progressive
                  weight gain over time among breast cancer survivors (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>,<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>), and the prevalence increases longitudinally (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). The Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS) highlighted a statistically significant quadratic trend in increasing
                  obesity prevalence in breast cancer survivors over time (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). Level of weight gain is greater than that observed in age-matched healthy women without breast cancer in some (<a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>) but not all populations (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>).
               </p>
               <p id="p-8" class="indent">The objective of this systematic review and meta-analysis was to determine whether weight gain (≥5.0% body weight) compared
                  with maintenance (&lt;±5.0%) measured at least one year post–primary breast cancer diagnosis is associated with increased risk
                  of all-cause mortality in women age 18 years or older diagnosed with stage I-IIIC breast cancer. We further explored the association
                  between weight gain after diagnosis and all-cause mortality, stratifying by level of weight gain and BMI at diagnosis.
               </p>
               <div class="section methods" id="sec-5">
                  <div class="section-nav"><a href="#abstract-1" title="Abstract" class="prev-section-link"><span>Previous Section</span></a><a href="#sec-18" title="Results" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  <h2 class="">Methods</h2>
                  <div id="sec-6" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Selection Criteria</h3>
                     <p id="p-9">All prospective and historical cohort studies and clinical trials measuring weight gain after breast cancer diagnosis and
                        all-cause/breast cancer–specific mortality or breast cancer recurrence were considered for inclusion. Selection criteria were:
                        female, age 18 years or older, with previous diagnosis of stage I-IIIC breast cancer (all histological subtypes) (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-7" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Primary Comparison</h3>
                     <p id="p-10">Weight maintenance was defined as fluctuations less than 5.0% above or below usual weight (&lt;±5.0% change body weight) to account
                        for baseline body size differences, usual weight variation, and measurement error and to provide a proportionate measure of
                        weight change. A 5.0% weight change is considered clinically meaningful (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>), and a cut point of 5.0% was consistent with previously reported data. Weight gain was calculated as the difference between
                        postdiagnosis and usual body weight either: 1) recalled weight prior to diagnosis or 2) measured at diagnosis. A minimum timeframe
                        of one year postdiagnosis for weight gain measurement was used to account for treatment-related weight fluctuation. The primary
                        comparison was body weight gain (≥5.0%) vs weight maintenance (&lt;±5.0% change).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-8" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Secondary Comparisons</h3>
                     <p id="p-11">Two secondary comparisons were conducted: 1) moderate weight gain (5%-10.0%) vs weight maintenance (&lt;±5.0% change), and 2)
                        high weight gain (&gt;10.0%) vs weight maintenance (&lt;±5.0% change). We were unable to conduct the following secondary comparisons
                        because of insufficient data: progressive weight gain (increased weight ≥5.0% at each of at least 2 time points) vs weight
                        maintenance (&lt;±5.0% body weight change); body fat percentage gain (≥5.0%), vs maintenance (&lt;±5.0% body fat percentage change).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-9" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Primary Outcome</h3>
                     <p id="p-12">The primary outcome was all-cause mortality, which is less prone to bias in classifying cause of death compared with disease-specific
                        mortality (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). All-cause mortality was measured as crude mortality rate (total number of deaths/mid-interval population at follow-up),
                        and all-cause mortality hazard defined as crude mortality rate at follow-up time conditional on survival to follow-up time.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-10" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Secondary Outcomes</h3>
                     <p id="p-13">Secondary outcomes were: 1) breast cancer–specific mortality rate (total number of deaths assigned to breast cancer/midinterval
                        population at follow-up) and breast cancer–specific hazard at follow-up; 2) breast cancer recurrence rate (total number of
                        breast cancer recurrences/mid-interval population at least 1 year postdiagnosis) and breast cancer recurrence hazard.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-11" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Search Methods</h3>
                     <p id="p-14">Detailed MeSH terms used to develop the search strategy are presented in the <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1093/jnci/djv275/-/DC1">Supplementary Materials</a> (available online). Electronic databases were searched for identification of abstracts and articles meeting selection criteria,
                        including PubMed (1966-present), EMBASE (1974 to present), CINAHL (1982 to present), and Web of Science. Language and publication
                        status were not restricted. All original articles, previous reviews, and systematic reviews were evaluated for relevant references.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-12" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Data Collection and Analysis</h3>
                     <p id="p-15">A primary reviewer screened electronic databases for relevant titles in collaboration with a medical librarian. Abstracts
                        were selected that met inclusion criteria and screened for selection of full text, original articles if relevant or where
                        eligibility was unclear. All full-text articles that met selection criteria were included. Articles that were excluded were
                        recorded in a table of characteristics of excluded studies, with justification. Full text articles were searched for relevant
                        references to include as additional studies. Inclusion of potential studies was vetted by a second reviewer. Any disagreement
                        was resolved by discussion among reviewers.
                     </p>
                     <p id="p-16" class="indent">Study characteristics were tabulated using a data extraction form. Study protocol papers were obtained for clarification of
                        exposure and outcome assessment in primary studies, as required. If a study reported multiple measures of body size, body
                        weight change was prioritized. Outcome data were extracted from each included study for use in meta-analysis. If results of
                        a study were reported in multiple publications, the most recent publication that included the relevant information was included.
                        If a study reported on multiple outcomes (including multiple cancers), data were only included that met eligibility criteria.
                        If outcomes were measured at multiple time points, the time point closest to average was used for assessment of the primary
                        outcome.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-13" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Assessment of Risk of Bias</h3>
                     <p id="p-17">Methodological quality of each selected publication was evaluated to determine study validity. Studies were appraised for
                        observing guidelines for Strengthening the Reporting of Observational Studies in Epidemiology (STROBE) (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). Selection criteria and sampling fractions were used to assess sample representativeness. Bias was systematically evaluated
                        and recorded in a risk of bias table based on the Cochrane Collaboration ‘Risk of bias’ tool (‘high risk of bias,’ ‘low risk
                        of bias,’ or ‘unclear’) (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>), and the Newcastle-Ottawa Scale (NOS) for assessing quality of nonrandomized studies in meta-analyses (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). Method of random assignment was not assessed because participants were not randomly assigned to weight change exposure
                        groups. Criteria used to assess risk of bias are detailed in the <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1093/jnci/djv275/-/DC1">Supplementary Materials</a> (available online).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-14" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Measures of Exposure and Outcome</h3>
                     <p id="p-18">Studies were evaluated for clinical relevance including magnitude and precision of outcome estimates. Outcomes were assessed
                        as dichotomous (died/alive; recurrence/no recurrence). Effect measures included hazard ratio (HR) or relative risk (RR) reported
                        with 95% confidence intervals. Both median survival and survival curves were considered in assessment of survival.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-15" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Statistical Analysis</h3>
                     <p id="p-19">Summary hazard ratios (HRs) and 95% confidence intervals (CIs) were generated using fixed effects models with reference manager
                        software (RevMan, version 5.2). Fixed effects meta-analysis was utilized because of the small number of studies included,
                        making estimation of between-subject variance more likely to be imprecise (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>); there was also some evidence that results for smaller studies were symmetrically different from larger studies (<a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1093/jnci/djv275/-/DC1">Supplementary Figure 1</a>, available online), which can lead to exacerbation of biased estimates (<a id="xref-ref-46-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). We explored sources of heterogeneity using stratification and repeated the analysis using random effects as an additional
                        sensitivity analysis (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). All tests were two-sided, and a <em>P</em> value of less than .05 was considered statistically significant. Both methodological heterogeneity (similarity of study designs,
                        participants, exposure, and outcome), and statistical heterogeneity (variability of reported outcomes) were assessed. The
                        proportion of variability explained by heterogeneity rather than chance was quantified using Chi<sup>2</sup> and I<sup>2</sup> statistics. Meta-analysis was conducted for methodologically homogenous studies where participants, exposures, and outcomes
                        were judged to be sufficiently similar. Heterogeneity was classified as low (0%-25.0%), medium (26%-75.0%), or high (&gt;75.0%)
                        (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). A qualitative analysis was conducted if high heterogeneity was present. Publication bias was not evaluated because of a
                        total of fewer than 10 included studies for each outcome (recurrence, breast cancer–specific, and all-cause mortality). For
                        descriptive purposes, a funnel plot is presented for studies contributing to the main comparison of interest in <a href="http://jnci.oxfordjournals.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1093/jnci/djv275/-/DC1">Supplementary Figure 1</a> (available online).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-16" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Subgroup Analysis</h3>
                     <p id="p-20">Subgroup analyses were defined a priori and tested effect modification with biological plausibility. We explored the effects
                        of weight gain on all-cause mortality by level of weight gain (moderate or 5%-10.0%; high or &gt;10.0%) and BMI at diagnosis
                        (&lt;25; ≥25kg/m<sup>2</sup>). We were unable to conduct subgroup analyses by cancer treatment type, menopausal status, hormone receptor status, or follow-up
                        duration because of insufficient data. As the comparison of interest was the effect of positive energy balance on cancer and
                        mortality outcomes, we did not assess the effects of weight loss.
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-17" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Sensitivity Analysis</h3>
                     <p id="p-21">Sensitivity analysis was performed to explore the variation between studies by including or excluding studies based on study
                        methodological quality (high/low risk of bias). Summary statistics were presented for homogenous results. Data were presented
                        by subgroup or individual trials if there was evidence for heterogeneity, with discussion of possible reasons for heterogeneity.
                        We also explored heterogeneity by year of study entry to account for changes in breast cancer treatments over time, excluding
                        studies 1) prior to 2000 and 2) after 2000.
                     </p>
                  </div>
               </div>
               <div class="section" id="sec-18">
                  <div class="section-nav"><a href="#sec-5" title="Methods" class="prev-section-link"><span>Previous Section</span></a><a href="#sec-35" title="Discussion" class="next-section-link"><span>Next Section</span></a></div>
                  <h2 class="">Results</h2>
                  <p id="p-22">Included studies were identified up to December 2014 (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). Twelve studies reported in nine publications (23 832 participants) met inclusion criteria for analysis of mortality or
                     breast cancer recurrence outcomes. MeSH search terms identified 729 studies from four search databases. Following removal
                     of duplicates, 424 studies were reviewed at title level, 261 at abstract level, and 70 at full text level. Two studies were
                     identified after the initial search (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Reasons for exclusion were: participants were not female, were younger than age 18 years, or had not been diagnosed with
                     stage I-IIIC breast cancer; there was no measure of weight change at least six months postchemotherapy or one year postdiagnosis;
                     there was no measure of body weight or BMI; the outcome was not all-cause or breast cancer mortality or breast cancer recurrence;
                     the study was not a clinical or cohort study; and the study was not conducted in humans. Search results are summarized in
                     <a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a>. All included studies were peer-reviewed and published in academic journals (<a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50–55</a>).
                  </p>
                  <div id="T1" class="table pos-float">
                     <div class="table-inline">
                        <div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                              <li><a href="djv275/T1.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                              <li><a class="in-nw" href="djv275/T1.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                           </ul>
                        </div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> 
                        <p id="p-23" class="first-child">Characteristics of studies*</p>
                        <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>
                     </div>
                  </div>
                  <div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd">
                     <div class="fig-inline"><a href="djv275/F1.expansion.html"><img alt="Figure 1." src="djv275/F1.small.gif" /></a><div class="callout"><span>View larger version:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                              <li><a href="djv275/F1.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                              <li><a class="in-nw" href="djv275/F1.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                           </ul>
                           <ul class="fig-services">
                              <li class="ppt-link"><a href="/powerpoint/107/12/djv275/F1">Download as PowerPoint Slide</a></li>
                           </ul>
                        </div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> 
                        <p id="p-25" class="first-child">PRISMA flow diagram. BC = breast cancer; BMI = body mass index.</p>
                        <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>
                     </div>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-19" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Study Design</h3>
                     <p id="p-26">Seven studies were prospective, including observational cohort designs (<a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>); one study included data from two clinical trials (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Two reports by Caan et al. (<a id="xref-ref-28-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>) presented different outcomes from the same studies. The 2012 report presented mortality data and included a pooled analysis
                        from four cohorts, whereas the 2006 report presented breast cancer recurrence data from two out of these four cohorts. For
                        the remainder of this report, we refer to the number of independent studies as opposed to the number of publications that
                        included meta-analyzed or pooled data. Three retrospective cohort studies were reviewed (<a id="xref-ref-50-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>), however the results of two Cox proportional hazards analyses could not be included in meta-analysis because they did not
                        report effect estimates for percent weight gain and all-cause mortality or breast cancer recurrence outcome (<a id="xref-ref-51-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-54-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-20" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Participants</h3>
                     <p id="p-27">All included studies enrolled women age 18 years or older diagnosed with stage I-IIIC breast cancer. In five studies, women
                        were enrolled who were undergoing adjuvant chemotherapy for breast cancer (<a id="xref-ref-51-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-53-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53–55</a>). Women in all studies were both pre- and postmenopausal, with prediagnosis BMI varying from underweight to obese. Measurement
                        of prediagnosis or at-diagnosis body weight was at or within two months of breast cancer diagnosis. Three studies conducted
                        stratified analysis by menopausal status (<a id="xref-ref-50-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>) and six by baseline BMI (<a id="xref-ref-28-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>), although one study was not included in subgroup analyses given that results were reported as ‘not significant’ (<a id="xref-ref-55-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-21" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Weight Gain Exposure Measurement</h3>
                     <p id="p-28">Exposure was defined as weight maintenance (&lt;±5.0% body weight) and weight gain (≥5.0% body weight) in six studies (<a id="xref-ref-28-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). Five studies further categorized weight gain into 5% to 10.0% and above 10.0% weight gain (<a id="xref-ref-28-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). The definition of weight gain in one study was unclear (<a id="xref-ref-55-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>); weight gain was analyzed as a continuous variable in one study that was not included in the meta-analysis (<a id="xref-ref-54-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). One study reported tertiles of weight gain and a summary measure of at least 5kg weight gain. For a mean BMI of 25kg/m<sup>2</sup> reported in the study, 3 to 4kg weight gain corresponds to approximately 5.0% body weight (<a id="xref-ref-31-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>) based on average height of postmenopausal women (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>). Weight maintenance was defined as ±2kg. We included tertiles of greater than 2kg gain in calculation of weight gain (2–6kg
                        as moderate-level weight gain, and &gt;6kg as high-level weight gain). One study presenting data from two clinical trials defined
                        weight gain as above vs below median weight change, which corresponded to an average 5.0% body weight change (<a id="xref-ref-53-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Percentage BMI change was the unit of measurement for one study, although we were unable to convert these measures to percent
                        weight change (<a id="xref-ref-51-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). The timing of exposure assessment varied between studies but all had initial weight measurements representing prediagnosis
                        or usual weight (within a short period of diagnosis), with follow-up body weight measured at least one year postdiagnosis
                        or six months postchemotherapy. The median follow-up time for measurement of weight gain exposure was 1.5 years. Where reported,
                        the underlying time metric for Cox proportional hazards regression was time since diagnosis (<a id="xref-ref-28-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Studies with multiple body weight change measures accounted for multiple measures using proportional hazards regression
                        with time-varying covariates (<a id="xref-ref-52-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>).
                     </p>
                     <p id="p-29" class="indent">Exposure ascertainment was by self-report questionnaire in five studies (<a id="xref-ref-28-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>), a combination of structured interview and self-report questionnaire (<a id="xref-ref-31-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>), objective measures and medical record review (<a id="xref-ref-51-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>), retrospective self-report and objective measures (<a id="xref-ref-33-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>), objective measures only (<a id="xref-ref-51-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-53-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>), and medical record review only (<a id="xref-ref-50-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-54-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-22" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Outcomes</h3>
                     <p id="p-30">All-cause mortality was the primary outcome in nine studies (<a id="xref-ref-28-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52–54</a>). Nine studies included breast cancer–specific mortality as an outcome (<a id="xref-ref-28-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52–54</a>). Breast cancer recurrence was the primary outcome in five studies (<a id="xref-ref-33-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-51-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). Record linkage was used to ascertain outcomes in six studies (<a id="xref-ref-28-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>), while medical record review was used for seven studies (<a id="xref-ref-28-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-33-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-50-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a id="xref-ref-54-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). Two studies validated self-report of breast cancer recurrence with confirmation by a study pathologist (<a id="xref-ref-33-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). Method of outcome assessment was unclear for three studies (<a id="xref-ref-51-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-53-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Timing of outcome assessment from the second weight measurement varied from one to two (<a id="xref-ref-50-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>), two (<a id="xref-ref-55-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>), three to five (<a id="xref-ref-33-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>), six (<a id="xref-ref-31-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-53-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>), and eight years (<a id="xref-ref-28-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>) and was not specified in one study (<a id="xref-ref-54-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-23" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Excluded Studies</h3>
                     <p id="p-31">Thirteen studies appeared to meet eligibility criteria but were excluded on detailed review. Two studies were excluded (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) as they described results from the same trials that were included in a subsequent pooled cohort study (<a id="xref-ref-28-15" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). A further four studies measured weight gain during chemotherapy (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>,<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57–59</a>). Three studies measured weight gain across the lifespan from the ages of 18 to 20 years until follow-up body weight measurement
                        (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60–62</a>). One study did not measure all-cause mortality as the primary outcome (<a id="xref-ref-38-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>); weight gain was not the primary exposure in one excluded study (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>). One study measured absolute weight and BMI change from prechemotherapy to postchemotherapy (immediate, 6-month, and annually
                        through 5 years) and both breast cancer recurrence and overall survival outcomes. However, absolute weight and BMI changes
                        were used as the exposure of interest, including both weight loss and weight gain. By combining weight loss/gain, overall
                        relative weight change at five years was 3.5% gain, which was not associated with overall survival (<em>P</em> = .128) (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>).
                     </p>
                  </div>
                  <div id="sec-24" class="subsection">
                     <h3>Primary Outcome Measure</h3>
                     <div id="sec-25" class="subsection">
                        <h4>All-Cause Mortality</h4>
                        <p id="p-32">Eight studies were included in a meta-analysis of the primary outcome, all-cause mortality (<a id="xref-ref-28-16" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a id="xref-ref-31-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a id="xref-ref-52-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>,<a id="xref-ref-53-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>). Four studies reported on breast cancer recurrence and were not included (<a id="xref-ref-33-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a id="xref-ref-51-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>,<a id="xref-ref-55-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). One study presented outcomes as <em>P</em> values only (<em>P</em> = .2 for a positive association with mortality) with no reporting of the number of events in each category, therefore we
                           were unable to include the results (<a id="xref-ref-54-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). Of the included studies, all but two (<a id="xref-ref-53-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>) presented effect estimates for categories of weight gain (5%-10.0%; ≥10.0%) or stratified by study in pooled analyses (<a id="xref-ref-28-17" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Our first summary measure summarizes results from each of these analyses. The hazard of mortality for gaining 5.0% or higher
                           body weight was 1.12 times the hazard of mortality for maintaining body weight (HR = 1.12, 95% CI = 1.03 to 1.22, <em>P</em> = .01). There was a moderate level of study heterogeneity (I<sup>2</sup> = 55.0%).
                        </p>
                        <div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd">
                           <div class="fig-inline"><a href="djv275/F2.expansion.html"><img alt="Figure 2." src="djv275/F2.small.gif" /></a><div class="callout"><span>View larger version:</span><ul class="callout-links">
                                    <li><a href="djv275/F2.expansion.html">In this window</a></li>
                                    <li><a class="in-nw" href="djv275/F2.expansion.html">In a new window</a></li>
                                 </ul>
                                 <ul class="fig-services">
                                    <li class="ppt-link"><a href="/powerpoint/107/12/djv275/F2">Download as PowerPoint Slide</a></li>
                                 </ul>
                              </div>
                           </div>
                           <div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 2.</span> 
                              <p id="p-33" class="first-child">Forrest plot: Fixed effects meta-analysis of the association between weight gain ≥ 5.0% and all-cause mortality. The black
                                 squares and horizontal lines represent study-specific hazard ratios (HR) and their 95% confidence intervals (CI). The <em>P</em>-values were calculated using Z- score for overall effect and Chi<sup>2</sup> test for heterogeneity. All tests were two-sided (<em>P</em> &lt; .05). KG kilograms; LACE = Life After Cancer Epidemiology; NHS = Nurse’s Health Study; SBCSS = Shanghai Breast Cancer Survival
                                 Study; WG Weight Gain; WHEL = Women’s Healthy Eating and Living. 
                              </p>
